[mathjaxinline]a_2=[/mathjaxinline]


Let [mathjaxinline]j[/mathjaxinline] denote [mathjaxinline]3t^4 + 3t[/mathjaxinline]. Can you write [mathjaxinline]j[/mathjaxinline] as a linear combination [mathjaxinline]j = a_1 f + a_2 g[/mathjaxinline]? If so, enter possible values for [mathjaxinline]a_1[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]a_2[/mathjaxinline]. If not, enter No in both boxes.

[mathjaxinline]a_1=[/mathjaxinline]


[mathjaxinline]a_2=[/mathjaxinline]


Answer:

For the first question, a little bit of trial and error gives us that [mathjaxinline]h = 17f + 2g[/mathjaxinline].

In the second problem, we are asked to combine [mathjaxinline]f[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]g[/mathjaxinline] to make a polynomial with a quartic term (one that involves [mathjaxinline]t^4[/mathjaxinline]). There is no way to multiply [mathjaxinline]f[/mathjaxinline] or [mathjaxinline]g[/mathjaxinline] by a scalar to get a quartic term. It is true that [mathjaxinline]j = 3t\cdot f + 0g,[/mathjaxinline] but [mathjaxinline]3t[/mathjaxinline] is not a scalar.

Let [mathjaxinline]v_1 = \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 3 \\ 1 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]v_2 = \left[\begin{array}{c} 3 \\ 10 \\ 2 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline] be vectors in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^3[/mathjaxinline].

Use row reduction to determine the answer to the following: Can you write [mathjaxinline]w = \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 5 \\ -1 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline] as a linear combination [mathjaxinline]w = a_1 v_1 + a_2 v_2[/mathjaxinline]? If so, enter possible values for [mathjaxinline]a_1[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]a_2[/mathjaxinline]. If not, enter No in both boxes.

[mathjaxinline]a_1=[/mathjaxinline]


[mathjaxinline]a_2=[/mathjaxinline]


Answer:

With the vectors [mathjaxinline]v_1[/mathjaxinline], [mathjaxinline]v_2[/mathjaxinline], and [mathjaxinline]w[/mathjaxinline], form an augmented matrix:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 3 & 1 \\ 3 & 10 & 5 \\ 1 & 2 & -1 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Multiply the first row by -3, and add this to the second row:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 3 & 1 \\ 0 & 1 & 2 \\ 1 & 2 & -1 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Multiply the first row by -1, and add this to the third row:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 3 & 1 \\ 0 & 1 & 2 \\ 0 & -1 & -2 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Add the second row to the third row:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 3 & 1 \\ 0 & 1 & 2 \\ 0 & 0 & 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Multiply the second row by -3, and add this to the first row:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 0 & -5 \\ 0 & 1 & 2 \\ 0 & 0 & 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

We've fully row reduced the matrix; we see that the system is consistent and that [mathjaxinline]a_1 = -5[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]a_2 = 2[/mathjaxinline]. (Note also that we have no free variables; our solution, given by the right side of the augmented matrix, is unique.)

You can verify your solution by computing [mathjaxinline]-5v_1 + 2v_2[/mathjaxinline]; you should get [mathjaxinline]w[/mathjaxinline].

Given vectors [mathjaxinline]v_1, v_2, \ldots v_ n[/mathjaxinline] in a vector space [mathjaxinline]V[/mathjaxinline] over a field [mathjaxinline]F[/mathjaxinline], we define the span of [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\}[/mathjaxinline] to be the set of all linear combinations of [mathjaxinline]v_1, v_2, \ldots v_ n[/mathjaxinline]. In other words,

[mathjax]\mathrm{Span}\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\} = \{ a_1 v_1 + a_2 v_2 + \ldots + a_ nv_ n : a_ i \in F \} .[/mathjax]

The span of the list with no vectors in it is defined to be [mathjaxinline]\{ \mathbf{0}\}[/mathjaxinline].

Let [mathjaxinline]v_1 = \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline], [mathjaxinline]v_2 = \left[\begin{array}{c} 0 \\ -1 \\ 2 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline], [mathjaxinline]v_3 = \left[\begin{array}{c} -1 \\ -2 \\ 3 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline], and [mathjaxinline]v_4 = \left[\begin{array}{c} -2 \\ -5 \\ 8 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline].

Use row reduction to determine if [mathjaxinline]w = \left[\begin{array}{c} 3 \\ 1 \\ 3 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline] is in the span of [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3; v_4 \}[/mathjaxinline]. Is [mathjaxinline]w \in \mathrm{Span}\{ v_1; v_2; v_3; v_4 \}[/mathjaxinline]?

Answer:

The question is equivalent to asking about the consistency of a system of linear equations given by the following augmented matrix:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cccc:c} 1 & 0 & -1 & -2 & 3 \\ 1 & -1 & -2 & -5 & 1 \\ 0 & 2 & 3 & 8 & 3 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

We will row reduce this matrix. Then we'll see if the row reduced augmented matrix represents a consistent system of equations. If so, then the above augmented matrix also represents a consistent system, and [mathjaxinline]w[/mathjaxinline] is in the span of the given set of vectors.

Multiply the first row by -1, and add this to the second row:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cccc:c} 1 & 0 & -1 & -2 & 3 \\ 0 & -1 & -1 & -3 & -2 \\ 0 & 2 & 3 & 8 & 3 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Multiply the second row by 2, and add this to the third row:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cccc:c} 1 & 0 & -1 & -2 & 3 \\ 0 & -1 & -1 & -3 & -2 \\ 0 & 0 & 1 & 2 & -1 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

We could stop here. Our matrix now has 3 pivots for its 3 rows. It must represent a consistent system of equations. Here we will continue the process of row reduction, but only for the purpose of elucidating our claim.
Add the third row to the second row:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cccc:c} 1 & 0 & -1 & -2 & 3 \\ 0 & -1 & 0 & -1 & -3 \\ 0 & 0 & 1 & 2 & -1 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Add the third row to the first row:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cccc:c} 1 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 2 \\ 0 & -1 & 0 & -1 & -3 \\ 0 & 0 & 1 & 2 & -1 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

For good measure, multiply the second row by -1:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cccc:c} 1 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 2 \\ 0 & 1 & 0 & 1 & 3 \\ 0 & 0 & 1 & 2 & -1 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

The above is consistent, as there is no row of all zeros followed by a non-zero in the right column. Therefore, [mathjaxinline]w[/mathjaxinline] is in the span of the given set of vectors.

Now determine if [mathjaxinline]u = \left[\begin{array}{c} 301.34 \\ -234.8 \\ \pi \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline] is in the span of [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3; v_4 \}[/mathjaxinline]. Try to avoid doing any additional calculation!

Is [mathjaxinline]u \in \mathrm{Span}\{ v_1; v_2; v_3; v_4 \}[/mathjaxinline]?

Answer:

The question is equivalent to asking about the consistency of a system of linear equations given by the following augmented matrix: [mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cccc:c} 1 & 0 & -1 & -2 & 301.34 \\ 1 & -1 & -2 & -5 & -234.8 \\ 0 & 2 & 3 & 8 & \pi \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline].

However, we don't need to do any new row-reduction – from our previous calculation, we know that when we row-reduce the coefficient part, there will be no row of all zeros in the row-reduced coefficient matrix (put another way, there will be a pivot in every row). Hence, in the augmented matrix, there can be no row of all zeros followed by a non-zero in the right-most column. The system must be consistent, so [mathjaxinline]u[/mathjaxinline] is in [mathjaxinline]\mathrm{Span}\{ v_1; v_2; v_3; v_4 \}[/mathjaxinline]. This will be true for any vector in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^3[/mathjaxinline], not just [mathjaxinline]u[/mathjaxinline].

Given vectors [mathjaxinline]v_1, v_2, \ldots v_ n[/mathjaxinline] in a vector space [mathjaxinline]V[/mathjaxinline] over a field [mathjaxinline]F[/mathjaxinline], we say that the list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\}[/mathjaxinline] spans [mathjaxinline]V[/mathjaxinline] if the span of the list of vectors is all of [mathjaxinline]V[/mathjaxinline].

If [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\}[/mathjaxinline] is a list of vectors in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline], the list spans [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline] if and only if the matrix

[mathjax]A = \left[ \begin{array}{cccc} | & | & & | \\ v_1 & v_2 & \cdots & v_ n \\ | & | & & | \end{array} \right][/mathjax]

row reduces to have a pivot in every row.

If [mathjaxinline]n<m[/mathjaxinline], then it is impossible for the list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\}[/mathjaxinline] to span [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline].

Consider the following vectors in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline].

Is [mathjaxinline]u[/mathjaxinline] an element of [mathjaxinline]\mathrm{Span}\{ v_1; v_2\}[/mathjaxinline]?

Is [mathjaxinline]w[/mathjaxinline] an element of [mathjaxinline]\mathrm{Span}\{ v_1; v_2\}[/mathjaxinline]?

Is every vector in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline] an element of [mathjaxinline]\mathrm{Span}\{ v_1; v_2\}[/mathjaxinline]?

Does the list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2\}[/mathjaxinline] span [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline]?

Answer:

From the parallelogram grid, we can see that [mathjaxinline]u = -2v_1 - 2v_2[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]w = 2v_1 + 4v_2[/mathjaxinline], so both are in the span of [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1;v_2\}[/mathjaxinline]. Indeed, every vector in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline] will be somewhere on this grid, and therefore will be in [mathjaxinline]\mathrm{Span}\{ v_1;v_2\}[/mathjaxinline]. Therefore this list spans [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline].

Does every list of two non-zero vectors in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline] span [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline]?

Answer:

If the two vectors are scalar multiples of each other, then they lie along the same line, and their span cannot include anything off that line. For instance, in the following picture, [mathjaxinline]w[/mathjaxinline] is not a linear combination of [mathjaxinline]v_1[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]v_2[/mathjaxinline], so [mathjaxinline]w[/mathjaxinline] is not in the span of [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2\}[/mathjaxinline] – the list does not span all of [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline].

  • List A: [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 7 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 14 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} -3 \\ -21 \end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline]

  • List B: [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 \end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline]

  • List C: [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 5 \\ 7 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline]

  • List D: [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \\ 4\end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 3 \\ 5 \\ 7\end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 12 \\ 23 \\ 35 \\ 58\end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline]

  • List E: [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 0 \\ 4\end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 13 \\ 0 \\ 7\end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 1 \\ 0 \\ -3\end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 15 \\ -2 \\ 0 \\ 2\end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 5 \\ 3 \\ 0 \\ 11\end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline]

  • List F: [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 3 \\ 8 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 6 \\ 16 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 8 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 1 \\ 8 \end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline]

Given above are six lists of vectors. Without doing any row reduction, you should be able to pick out three lists that do not span their respective [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline] spaces. Which three?

A B C D E F

Answer:

List A has three vectors that are scalar multiples of each other; they all lie on the same line. Thus any linear combination of them also lies on the line, so they cannot span all of [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline].
List D contains three vectors. Since these vectors are in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^4[/mathjaxinline], there aren't enough of them to span the whole space.
List E has five vectors in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^4[/mathjaxinline]. This may seem promising, but notice that all five of them have zero as the third entry. Any vector in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^4[/mathjaxinline] with a non-zero third entry cannot be expressed as a linear combination of these vectors, so they don't span all of [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^4[/mathjaxinline].

Feel free to discuss this learning sequence in the forum. Before creating a new post, please first check to see if there is already a question or discussion addressing your topic.

At the end of this sequence, and after some practice, you should be able to:

  • Compute the product of a matrix with a vector in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^ n[/mathjaxinline].

  • Solve matrix-vector equations.

  • Relate matrix-vector equations to spans and linear combinations.

For time budgeting purposes, this sequence has 4 videos totaling 29 minutes, plus some questions.

From this point forward, you should feel free to use technology to do row reduction of matrices. All other computations should still be done by hand, for now.

Here are a couple of websites that will do row reduction for you:

Linear Algebra Toolkit

Wolfram Alpha

If [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] is an [mathjaxinline]m\times n[/mathjaxinline] matrix

[mathjax]A = \left[ \begin{array}{cccc} | & | & & | \\ v_1 & v_2 & \cdots & v_ n \\ | & | & & | \end{array} \right],[/mathjax]

and [mathjaxinline]x[/mathjaxinline] is the vector [mathjaxinline]x = \left[\begin{array}{c} a_1 \\ a_2 \\ \vdots \\ a_ n \end{array} \right],[/mathjaxinline] then the product [mathjaxinline]Ax[/mathjaxinline] is defined to be

[mathjax]Ax = a_1 v_1 + a_2 v_2 + \ldots + a_ n v_ n.[/mathjax]

Suppose [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] is a [mathjaxinline]6\times 4[/mathjaxinline] real matrix. In order for the product [mathjaxinline]Ax[/mathjaxinline] to be defined, the vector [mathjaxinline]x[/mathjaxinline] must live in what space?

[mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^4[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^6[/mathjaxinline] Either of these is fine

The resulting product [mathjaxinline]Ax[/mathjaxinline] will be an element of which space?

[mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^4[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^6[/mathjaxinline] Either of these is fine

Answer:

For the matrix-vector product to be defined, the number of columns of the first matrix must equal the number of entries in the vector (otherwise, the mechanics just don't work). Our matrix [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] has 4 columns, so [mathjaxinline]x[/mathjaxinline] must then live in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^4[/mathjaxinline].
Furthermore, if a matrix-vector product is defined, the result will have the same number of rows as the matrix. Our product [mathjaxinline]Ax[/mathjaxinline] will therefore be a vector living in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^6[/mathjaxinline].

Let

[mathjax]A = \left[ \begin{array}{cccc} 2 & 1 & 0 & 1\\ 0 & 3 & -1 & -1 \\ 5 & 0 & 3 & 1 \end{array} \right] ,[/mathjax]

and let [mathjaxinline]x = \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \\ 4 \end{array} \right].[/mathjaxinline] What is [mathjaxinline]Ax[/mathjaxinline]?

To enter a vector in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^ n[/mathjaxinline] such as [mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline], you can either:

  • enter it as you would a [mathjaxinline]3\times 1[/mathjaxinline] matrix: [[1],[2],[3]]

  • or, you can enter it as <1,2,3>

Use decimals only.

Let [mathjaxinline]y = \left[\begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 0 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{array} \right].[/mathjaxinline] What is [mathjaxinline]Ay[/mathjaxinline]?

Answer:

[mathjaxinline]Ax = 1 \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 0 \\ 5 \end{array} \right] + 2 \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 3 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] + 3 \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ -1 \\ 3 \end{array} \right] + 4 \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ -1 \\ 1 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

[mathjaxinline]= \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 0 \\ 5 \end{array} \right] + \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 6 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] + \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ -3 \\ 9 \end{array} \right] + \left[ \begin{array}{c} 4 \\ -4 \\ 4 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

[mathjaxinline]= \left[ \begin{array}{c} 8 \\ -1 \\ 18 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

[mathjaxinline]Ay = 0 \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 0 \\ 5 \end{array} \right] + 0 \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 3 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] + 1 \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ -1 \\ 3 \end{array} \right] + 0 \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ -1 \\ 1 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

[mathjaxinline]= \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] + \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] + \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ -1 \\ 3 \end{array} \right] + \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

[mathjaxinline]= \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ -1 \\ 3 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Note the special nature of vector [mathjaxinline]y[/mathjaxinline]. We can see that the product [mathjaxinline]Ay[/mathjaxinline] will equal the third column of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] for not just this matrix, but any matrix [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] with four columns.

True or false: If [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] is an [mathjaxinline]m\times n[/mathjaxinline] matrix

[mathjax]A = \left[ \begin{array}{cccc} | & | & & | \\ v_1 & v_2 & \cdots & v_ n \\ | & | & & | \end{array} \right],[/mathjax]

and [mathjaxinline]x[/mathjaxinline] is any vector in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^ n[/mathjaxinline], then the resulting product [mathjaxinline]Ax[/mathjaxinline] must be in [mathjaxinline]\mathrm{Span}\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\}[/mathjaxinline].

Answer:

The span of a list of vectors is the set of all linear combinations of those vectors. [mathjaxinline]Ax[/mathjaxinline] is defined to be a linear combination of the columns of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline], and so it is in the span of those columns.

Let

[mathjax]A = \left[ \begin{array}{cc} 1 & 2 \\ 1 & 3 \\ 3 & -1 \end{array} \right] ,[/mathjax]

and let [mathjaxinline]v = \left[\begin{array}{c} 3 \\ 2 \\ 16 \end{array} \right].[/mathjaxinline] The equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] has exactly one solution – what is it?

Let [mathjaxinline]w = \left[\begin{array}{c} 3 \\ -4 \\ 7 \end{array} \right].[/mathjaxinline] With the same [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] as above, how many solutions does [mathjaxinline]Ax = w[/mathjaxinline] have?

Answer:

We will row reduce the augmented matrix:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 2 & 3 \\ 1 & 3 & 2\\ 3 & -1 & 16 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Multiply the first row by -1, and add this to the second row:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 2 & 3 \\ 0 & 1 & -1\\ 3 & -1 & 16 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Multiply the first row by -3, and add this to the third row:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 2 & 3 \\ 0 & 1 & -1\\ 0 & -7 & 7 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Multiply the second row by 7, and add this to the third row:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 2 & 3 \\ 0 & 1 & -1\\ 0 & 0 & 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Multiply the second row by -2, and add this to the first row:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 0 & 5 \\ 0 & 1 & -1\\ 0 & 0 & 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Our vector [mathjaxinline]x[/mathjaxinline] is given by the last column.

We can perform the same steps to answer the second question:

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 2 & 3 \\ 1 & 3 & -4\\ 3 & -1 & 7 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 2 & 3 \\ 0 & 1 & -7\\ 3 & -1 & 7 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 2 & 3 \\ 0 & 1 & -7\\ 0 & -7 & -2 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

[mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc:c} 1 & 2 & 3 \\ 0 & 1 & -7\\ 0 & 0 & -51 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

We can stop here. The last row of the augmented matrix implies the equation [mathjaxinline]x_1 \cdot 0 + x_2 \cdot 0 = -51[/mathjaxinline], which has no solution. Therefore, the original equation has no solution.

Let [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] be a [mathjaxinline]3\times 4[/mathjaxinline] matrix with columns [mathjaxinline]v_1, v_2, v_3, v_4[/mathjaxinline]. That is,

[mathjax]A = \left[ \begin{array}{cccc} | & | & | & | \\ v_1 & v_2 & v_3 & v_4 \\ | & | & | & | \end{array} \right].[/mathjax]

Find a solution to the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = v_2[/mathjaxinline].

Answer:

We need not perform any row reduction here. Our desired result [mathjaxinline]v_2[/mathjaxinline] can be written

[mathjaxinline]0v_1 + 1 v_2 + 0 v_3 + 0 v_4[/mathjaxinline] , so

[mathjaxinline]x = \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline] is a solution to the equation.

Let [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] be a [mathjaxinline]4\times 3[/mathjaxinline] matrix. There is a specific vector [mathjaxinline]v[/mathjaxinline] for which you can conclude the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] has a solution, even without knowing anything else about [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline]. What is this vector [mathjaxinline]v[/mathjaxinline]?

Answer:

The equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline] always has at least one solution, namely [mathjaxinline]x = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]. Since [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] has 4 columns, [mathjaxinline]v[/mathjaxinline] is the zero vector in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^4[/mathjaxinline] specifically.

Let [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] be a [mathjaxinline]6\times 4[/mathjaxinline] matrix. The equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline] has the solution [mathjaxinline]x = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline], which we call the trivial solution. If there are no solutions other than the trivial solution, how many pivots must [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] have when row-reduced?

Answer:

The vector [mathjaxinline]x[/mathjaxinline] lives in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^4[/mathjaxinline], so there are four variables in this equation. If the trivial solution is the unique solution, then we cannot have free variables when the augmented matrix [mathjaxinline]\left[ \begin{array}{c:c} A & \mathbf{0}\end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline] is row-reduced. That means [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] will have a pivot in each of its four columns.

Let

[mathjax]A = \left[ \begin{array}{cccc} 1 & 1 & 0 & -1\\ 5 & 6 & -1 & 1 \end{array} \right].[/mathjax]

Find a list of vectors whose span is the set of solutions to [mathjaxinline]Ax = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline].

Enter the list of vectors below, separated by semicolons. For instance, to enter the list [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 \end{array} \right]; \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline], type <1,0,1>;<1,2,3>.

Answer:

We row reduce the matrix [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline]:

[mathjaxinline]\left[ \begin{array}{cccc} 1 & 1 & 0 & -1 \\ 5 & 6 & -1 & 1 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Multiply the first row by -5, and add this to the second:

[mathjaxinline]\left[ \begin{array}{cccc} 1 & 1 & 0 & -1 \\ 0 & 1 & -1 & 6 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Multiply the second row by -1, and add this to the first:

[mathjaxinline]\left[ \begin{array}{cccc} 1 & 0 & 1 & -7 \\ 0 & 1 & -1 & 6 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

Ordinarily, to solve a matrix equation, we would row reduce an augmented matrix, with the vector from the right hand side of the equation as the last column of the matrix. However, since the right hand side of the equation is the zero vector, row operations would have no effect on it. This tells us that if [mathjaxinline]A'[/mathjaxinline] is the row-reduced version of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline], then the set of solutions to [mathjaxinline]Ax = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline] is the same as the set of solutions to [mathjaxinline]A'x = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline].
We have 2 free variables; call them [mathjaxinline]x_3[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]x_4[/mathjaxinline]. We can get solutions to our equation by choosing any values we like for [mathjaxinline]x_3[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]x_4[/mathjaxinline], substituting, and solving for [mathjaxinline]x_1[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]x_2[/mathjaxinline]. We get [mathjaxinline]x_1 = -x_3 + 7x_4[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]x_2 = x_3 - 6x_4[/mathjaxinline].

Hence the general solution is

[mathjax]\left[ \begin{array}{c} x_1 \\ x_2 \\ x_3 \\ x_4 \end{array}\right] = \left[ \begin{array}{c} -x_3 + 7x_4 \\ x_3 - 6x_4 \\ x_3 \\ x_4 \end{array}\right] = x_3 \left[ \begin{array}{c} -1 \\ 1 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{array}\right] + x_4 \left[ \begin{array}{c} 7 \\ - 6 \\ 0 \\ 1 \end{array}\right],[/mathjax]

for any [mathjaxinline]x_3,x_4 \in \mathbb R[/mathjaxinline]. Hence the set of solutions is the span of [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} -1 \\ 1 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{array} \right]; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 7 \\ -6 \\ 0 \\ 1 \end{array} \right]\right\} .[/mathjaxinline]

(Note that this is not the only list that spans the solution set, but it is the one that is simplest to find.)

True or false: If [mathjaxinline]S[/mathjaxinline] is the solution set to [mathjaxinline]Ax = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline], then the solution set to [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] must be a translation of [mathjaxinline]S[/mathjaxinline].

Answer:

If the solution set to [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] is empty (i.e. if [mathjaxinline]v[/mathjaxinline] is not a linear combination of the columns of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline]), then it can't be a translation of [mathjaxinline]S[/mathjaxinline], which is sure to contain at least one solution (the zero vector).

If [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] is consistent, then the solution set will be a translation of [mathjaxinline]S[/mathjaxinline].

Proposition. If [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] is consistent, then the set of solutions to [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] is a translation of the set of solutions to [mathjaxinline]Ax = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline].

Let [mathjaxinline]S[/mathjaxinline] be the solution set to [mathjaxinline]Ax = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline], and suppose that [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] is consistent. Then there is some solution [mathjaxinline]x=x_0[/mathjaxinline]. We claim that the solution set to [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] is exactly [mathjaxinline]x_0 + S[/mathjaxinline].

First we show that every vector in the translation [mathjaxinline]x_0 + S[/mathjaxinline] is a solution to [mathjaxinline]Ax=v.[/mathjaxinline] Let [mathjaxinline]w \in x_0+S[/mathjaxinline]. Then [mathjaxinline]w = x_0 + y[/mathjaxinline] for some [mathjaxinline]y \in S[/mathjaxinline], so

[mathjax]Aw = A(x_0 + y) = Ax_0 + Ay.[/mathjax]

We know that [mathjaxinline]Ax_0 = v[/mathjaxinline] and, since [mathjaxinline]y \in S[/mathjaxinline], we also know that [mathjaxinline]Ay = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]. Hence [mathjaxinline]Aw = v[/mathjaxinline], so [mathjaxinline]w[/mathjaxinline] is a solution to [mathjaxinline]Ax = v.[/mathjaxinline]

Now we show that every solution to [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] is an element of [mathjaxinline]x_0 + S.[/mathjaxinline] Indeed, suppose that [mathjaxinline]w[/mathjaxinline] satisfies [mathjaxinline]Aw = v[/mathjaxinline], and consider the vector [mathjaxinline]y = w-x_0.[/mathjaxinline] We have

[mathjax]Ay = A(w-x_0) = Aw - Ax_0 = v-v = \mathbf{0}.[/mathjax]

Hence [mathjaxinline]y \in S[/mathjaxinline]. Therefore [mathjaxinline]w = x_0 + y \in x_0 + S.[/mathjaxinline] This proves the proposition.

Let [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] be a [mathjaxinline]m\times n[/mathjaxinline] matrix. A vector [mathjaxinline]v[/mathjaxinline] is in the span of the columns of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] if and only the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] has:

Answer:

The vector [mathjaxinline]v[/mathjaxinline] is in the span of the columns of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] if there is at least one way to write it as a linear combination of the columns of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline], or in other words if there is at least one [mathjaxinline]x[/mathjaxinline] which satisfies [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline].

Let [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] be a [mathjaxinline]m\times n[/mathjaxinline] matrix. The columns of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] span [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline] if and only if:

For every vector [mathjaxinline]v\in \mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline], the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] has exactly one solution For every vector [mathjaxinline]v\in \mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline], the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] has at least one solution For every vector [mathjaxinline]v\in \mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline], the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] has at most one solution

Answer:

In order for the columns of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] to span [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline], every vector [mathjaxinline]v\in \mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline] needs to be in the span of the columns. By the previous problem, this is equivalent to having at least one solution to the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax=v[/mathjaxinline] for every vector [mathjaxinline]v \in \mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline].

In light of the previous problem, for which sizes of matrix [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] would it be impossible for [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] to have at least one solution for every [mathjaxinline]v \in \mathbb R^5[/mathjaxinline]? Click all that apply.

[mathjaxinline]5\times 2[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]5\times 4[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]5\times 5[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]5\times 7[/mathjaxinline]

Answer:

If there are fewer than 5 columns, then it is impossible for the columns of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] to span [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^5[/mathjaxinline]. In that case, by the previous problem, the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax=v[/mathjaxinline] cannot be consistent for every [mathjaxinline]v\in \mathbb R^5[/mathjaxinline]; there must be some vectors [mathjaxinline]v\in \mathbb R^5[/mathjaxinline] for which it is inconsistent.

The two cases in which there are fewer columns than rows are the answer.

For any [mathjaxinline]m\times n[/mathjaxinline] vector [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline], vectors [mathjaxinline]w,x \in \mathbb R^ n[/mathjaxinline], and scalar [mathjaxinline]a[/mathjaxinline], we have that

[mathjax]A(w+x) = Aw + Ax[/mathjax]

and

[mathjax]A(ax) = a(Ax).[/mathjax]

Let [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] be a [mathjaxinline]4\times 3[/mathjaxinline] matrix. Suppose that [mathjaxinline]x = \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline] is a solution to the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline], and suppose that [mathjaxinline]x = \left[\begin{array}{c} 2 \\ -1 \\ 4 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline] is a solution to the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = w[/mathjaxinline].

Find a solution to the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = v+w[/mathjaxinline].

Answer:

We are given:

[mathjaxinline]A \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{array} \right] = v[/mathjaxinline]

[mathjaxinline]A \left[\begin{array}{c} 2 \\ -1 \\ 4 \end{array} \right] = w[/mathjaxinline]

Adding these equations, we get

[mathjaxinline]A \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{array} \right] + A \left[\begin{array}{c} 2 \\ -1 \\ 4 \end{array} \right] = v + w[/mathjaxinline]

[mathjaxinline]A \Bigg( \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{array} \right] + \left[\begin{array}{c} 2 \\ -1 \\ 4 \end{array} \right] \Bigg) = v + w[/mathjaxinline]

[mathjaxinline]A \left[\begin{array}{c} 3 \\ 1 \\ 7 \end{array} \right] = v + w[/mathjaxinline]

Let [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] be a [mathjaxinline]4\times 3[/mathjaxinline] matrix. Suppose that the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline] is consistent. Must the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = 4v[/mathjaxinline] be consistent?

Answer:

We can show it with algebra. Suppose [mathjaxinline]y[/mathjaxinline] is a solution to the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = v[/mathjaxinline]. Then [mathjaxinline]Ay = v[/mathjaxinline], so [mathjaxinline]4Ay = 4v[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]A (4y) = 4v.[/mathjaxinline] Thus [mathjaxinline]4y[/mathjaxinline] is a solution to the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = 4v[/mathjaxinline].

Intuitively, if we can produce [mathjaxinline]v[/mathjaxinline] by linear combination of the columns of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline], we can simply scale the weights of the linear combination to produce any scaling [mathjaxinline]cv[/mathjaxinline] of [mathjaxinline]v[/mathjaxinline].

Feel free to discuss this learning sequence in the forum. Before creating a new post, please first check to see if there is already a question or discussion addressing your topic.

At the end of this sequence, and after some practice, you should be able to:

  • Work with the definition of linear independence.

  • Determine whether a list of vectors is linearly independent.

  • State conditions on whether a list of vectors in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^ n[/mathjaxinline] can be linearly independent.

For time budgeting purposes, this sequence has 5 videos totaling 25 minutes, plus some questions.

Given a matrix [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline], consider the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline].

Regarding such equations, we have discussed two main questions: the existence question (does there exist at least one solution?) and the uniqueness question (is there at most one solution?). For this equation, one of these questions always has the same answer, no matter what [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] is. Which question does not always have the same answer?

Answer:

Because [mathjaxinline]A\mathbf{0}= \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline] always, the answer to the existence question is always "yes" for this equation. It's not always the case, however, that the zero vector is the only solution; for some matrices, some other combination of the columns of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] will also give zero.

Which of these is the definition of linearly dependent?

A list of vectors [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\}[/mathjaxinline] is linearly dependent if...

...there exist scalars [mathjaxinline]a_1, a_2, \ldots a_ n[/mathjaxinline], not all zero, such that [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + \ldots a_ nv_ n = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]. ...there exist scalars [mathjaxinline]a_1, a_2, \ldots a_ n[/mathjaxinline], all not zero, such that [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + \ldots a_ nv_ n = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]. Both of these are the definition.

Answer:

We know that if all the scalars equal zero, the equation [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + \ldots a_ nv_ n = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline] will be true. The idea of linear dependence is that we can choose a different set of scalars that also makes the equation true; i.e., a set of scalars that is not all zeros. (They may very well be all non-zero scalars, but this is not required.)

Definition. We say that a list of vectors [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\}[/mathjaxinline] in a vector space [mathjaxinline]V[/mathjaxinline] is linearly independent if the only way to pick scalars [mathjaxinline]a_1, a_2, \ldots a_ n[/mathjaxinline] such that [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + \ldots + a_ nv_ n = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline] is if [mathjaxinline]a_1 = a_2 = \ldots = a_ n = 0[/mathjaxinline].

Definition. A list of vectors [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\}[/mathjaxinline] in a vector space [mathjaxinline]V[/mathjaxinline] is linearly dependent if there are scalars [mathjaxinline]a_1, a_2, \ldots a_ n[/mathjaxinline], not all zero, such that [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + \ldots + a_ nv_ n = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline].

Consider the list of vectors [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3\}[/mathjaxinline], where

[mathjax]v_1 = \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ -1 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{array} \right], v_2 = \left[\begin{array}{c} 2 \\ -1 \\ 3 \\ 5 \end{array} \right], v_3 = \left[\begin{array}{c} -3 \\ 3 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{array} \right].[/mathjax]

Can you find scalars [mathjaxinline]a_1, a_2, a_3[/mathjaxinline], not all zero, such that [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2 v_2 + a_3 v_3 = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]? If so, enter an example of such scalars below. (Use decimals only, separated by commas. For instance, enter 3,2,5 if you want [mathjaxinline]a_1 = 3, a_2=2, a_3=5[/mathjaxinline].) If not, enter ‚ÄòNo'.

Is the list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] linearly dependent or linearly independent?

Answer:

We notice that [mathjaxinline]-3v_1 = v_3[/mathjaxinline], so that [mathjaxinline]3 v_1 + 0 v_2 + 1 v_3 = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]. Therefore, [mathjaxinline]a_1 = 3[/mathjaxinline], [mathjaxinline]a_2 = 0[/mathjaxinline], and [mathjaxinline]a_3 = 1[/mathjaxinline] is a possible set of scalars that satisfies the equation. (Any scalar multiple of that triple is also a set that satisfies the equation.)

The list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] is linearly dependent because, as we've just seen, there exist sets of coefficients, not all zero, that yield [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2 v_2 + a_3 v_3 = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline].

Consider the list of vectors [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3\}[/mathjaxinline], where

[mathjax]v_1 = \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ -1 \\ 3 \\ 4 \\ 5 \end{array} \right], v_2 = \left[\begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{array} \right], v_3 = \left[\begin{array}{c} -3 \\ 3 \\ 3 \\ 5 \\ 1 \end{array} \right].[/mathjax]

Can you find scalars [mathjaxinline]a_1, a_2, a_3[/mathjaxinline], not all zero, such that [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2 v_2 + a_3 v_3 = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]? If so, enter an example of such scalars below. (Use decimals only, separated by commas. For instance, enter 3,2,5 if you want [mathjaxinline]a_1 = 3, a_2=2, a_3=5[/mathjaxinline].) If not, enter ‚ÄòNo'.

Is the list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] linearly dependent or linearly independent?

Answer:

The first answer is immediately "yes" because we have the zero vector in our set; we can choose any non-zero value for [mathjaxinline]a_2[/mathjaxinline], and with [mathjaxinline]a_1 = 0[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]a_3 = 0[/mathjaxinline], the above equation will still be true. Because there exist sets of coefficients, not all zero, that make the above equation true, the list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] is linearly dependent.

Let [mathjaxinline]\mathbb {P}[/mathjaxinline] be the vector space of polynomials with real coefficients in the variable [mathjaxinline]t[/mathjaxinline]. Consider the list of vectors [mathjaxinline]\{ f; g\}[/mathjaxinline], where [mathjaxinline]f(t) = 1+t[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]g(t) = t+t^2[/mathjaxinline] are vectors in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb {P}[/mathjaxinline].

Can you find scalars [mathjaxinline]a_1, a_2[/mathjaxinline], not both zero, such that [mathjaxinline]a_1f + a_2 g = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]? If so, enter an example of such scalars below. If not, type ‚ÄòNo'.

Is the list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2\}[/mathjaxinline] linearly dependent or linearly independent?

Answer:

We cannot find scalars [mathjaxinline]a_1, a_2[/mathjaxinline], not both zero, such that [mathjaxinline]a_1f + a_2 g = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]. If [mathjaxinline]a_1 \ne 0[/mathjaxinline], then [mathjaxinline]a_1 f[/mathjaxinline] will have a non-zero constant term, and [mathjaxinline]a_2 g[/mathjaxinline] must have a zero constant term, so [mathjaxinline]a_1 f + a_2g[/mathjaxinline] will have a non-zero constant term and therefore not be the zero polynomial.

Similarly, if [mathjaxinline]a_2 \ne 0[/mathjaxinline], then [mathjaxinline]a_2 g[/mathjaxinline] will have a non-zero [mathjaxinline]t^2[/mathjaxinline] term, whereas [mathjaxinline]a_1 f[/mathjaxinline] cannot have a [mathjaxinline]t^2[/mathjaxinline] term, so [mathjaxinline]a_1 f + a_2g[/mathjaxinline] will have a non-zero [mathjaxinline]t^2[/mathjaxinline] term and therefore not be the zero polynomial.

Note that we cannot set [mathjaxinline]a_1 = -t[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]a_2 = 1[/mathjaxinline], since [mathjaxinline]-t[/mathjaxinline] is not a scalar.

Consider the vectors [mathjaxinline]v_1, v_2, v_3 \in \mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline] as given in this picture:

Which of the following lists are linearly independent? Click all that apply.

[mathjaxinline]\{ \mathbf{0}\}[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1\}[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1;v_2\}[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1;v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]\{ v_2;v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]\{ v_3;v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3\}[/mathjaxinline]

Answer:

The zero vector is not linearly independent as a list because [mathjaxinline]a_1 \mathbf{0}= \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline] for any scalar [mathjaxinline]a_1[/mathjaxinline].
[mathjaxinline]\{ v_1\}[/mathjaxinline] is linearly independent because [mathjaxinline]v_1[/mathjaxinline] is not the zero vector, and therefore [mathjaxinline]a_1 v_1 = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline] implies [mathjaxinline]a_1 = 0[/mathjaxinline].
It can be seen on the diagram that [mathjaxinline]v_1[/mathjaxinline] is not collinear with [mathjaxinline]v_2[/mathjaxinline], and likewise not with [mathjaxinline]v_3[/mathjaxinline], and so [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1;v_2\}[/mathjaxinline] is linearly independent and [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1;v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] is linearly independent.
[mathjaxinline]v_2[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]v_3[/mathjaxinline] appear to be collinear, so [mathjaxinline]\{ v_2;v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] is not linearly independent.
[mathjaxinline]v_3[/mathjaxinline] is of course collinear with itself, so [mathjaxinline]\{ v_3;v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] is not linearly independent.
Finally, since [mathjaxinline]\{ v_2;v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] is not linearly independent and is contained in [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1;v_2;v_3\}[/mathjaxinline], this last list cannot be linearly independent.

Proposition. Let [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\}[/mathjaxinline] be vectors in a vector space [mathjaxinline]V[/mathjaxinline]. The list is linearly dependent if and only if one of the vectors in the list can be expressed as a linear combination of the other vectors.

True or False: Suppose the list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3; v_4\}[/mathjaxinline] is linearly dependent. Then [mathjaxinline]v_4[/mathjaxinline] must be in the span of [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3\}[/mathjaxinline].

Answer:

The conclusion is too specific. We proved that if our list is linearly dependent, then at least one of the vectors will be in the span of the others. We can't claim that a particular vector in the list is in the span of the rest of the list, or that all vectors are in the spans of their list complements (without knowing more). Consider the problem on the previous tab with [mathjaxinline]v_1[/mathjaxinline], [mathjaxinline]v_2[/mathjaxinline], and [mathjaxinline]v_3[/mathjaxinline]: it appears that [mathjaxinline]v_3[/mathjaxinline] is not in the span of [mathjaxinline]v_1[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]v_2[/mathjaxinline], and yet the list of three is linearly dependent.

Let's think about the definition of linear independence. Recall that a list of vectors [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\}[/mathjaxinline] in a vector space [mathjaxinline]V[/mathjaxinline] is linearly independent if the only way to pick scalars [mathjaxinline]a_1, a_2, \ldots a_ n[/mathjaxinline] such that [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + \ldots + a_ nv_ n = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline] is if [mathjaxinline]a_1 = a_2 = \ldots = a_ n = 0[/mathjaxinline].

Which of the following would be a way to prove that a list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\}[/mathjaxinline] is linearly independent? Check all that apply.

Assume that [mathjaxinline]a_ i = 0[/mathjaxinline] for all [mathjaxinline]i[/mathjaxinline] from 1 to [mathjaxinline]n[/mathjaxinline], and show [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + \ldots + a_ nv_ n = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]. Assume that [mathjaxinline]a_ i \ne 0[/mathjaxinline] for all [mathjaxinline]i[/mathjaxinline] from 1 to [mathjaxinline]n[/mathjaxinline], and show [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + \ldots + a_ nv_ n \ne \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]. Assume that [mathjaxinline]a_ i \ne 0[/mathjaxinline] for at least one [mathjaxinline]i[/mathjaxinline] from 1 to [mathjaxinline]n[/mathjaxinline], and show [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + \ldots + a_ nv_ n \ne \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]. Assume that [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + \ldots + a_ nv_ n = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline], and show that [mathjaxinline]a_ i = 0[/mathjaxinline] for all [mathjaxinline]i[/mathjaxinline] from 1 to [mathjaxinline]n[/mathjaxinline]. Assume that [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + \ldots + a_ nv_ n \ne \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline], and show that [mathjaxinline]a_ i \ne 0[/mathjaxinline] for at least one [mathjaxinline]i[/mathjaxinline] from 1 to [mathjaxinline]n[/mathjaxinline].

Answer:

The approach in choice 1 won't prove linear independence, because the conclusion will be true regardless of the vectors in the list.
The approach in choice 2 won't prove linear independence, because even if our vector list passes the test in choice 2, there may yet exist some set of coefficients, not all zero but containing at least one zero, that gives a zero linear combination (i.e., the list could still be linearly dependent).
The approach in choice 3 will prove linear independence. If it's true that every set of coefficients, not all zero, will give a non-zero linear combination, then the only way to get a zero linear combination is with all zero coefficients, which is the definition of linear independence.
The approach in choice 4 will prove linear independence. It simply uses the definition. (Notice that choices 3 and 4 are just contrapositives of each other.)
The approach in choice 5 won't prove linear independence, because the conclusion will be true regardless of the vectors in the list. (This is the contrapositive of choice 1.)

Is the linear independence of a list of vectors an existence question, or a uniqueness question?

Answer:

A list of vectors is linearly independent if the only way to write [mathjaxinline]\mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline] as a linear combination of the list is when all coefficients are zero. This is a uniqueness statement.

Use row reduction to determine how many solutions the equation [mathjaxinline]Ax = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline] has, where

[mathjax]A = \left[\begin{array}{ccc} 1 & 2 & 3 \\ -1 & 1 & 3 \\ 3 & 3 & 3 \\ 4 & 1 & -2 \\ 5 & 3 & 1 \end{array} \right].[/mathjax]

Is the list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] linearly independent, where the [mathjaxinline]v_ i[/mathjaxinline] are given below?

[mathjax]v_1 = \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ -1 \\ 3 \\ 4 \\ 5 \end{array} \right], v_2 = \left[\begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 1 \\ 3 \\ 1 \\ 3 \end{array} \right], v_3 = \left[\begin{array}{c} 3 \\ 3 \\ 3 \\ -2 \\ 1 \end{array} \right].[/mathjax]

Answer: First, we recall that there must always exist at least one solution to [mathjaxinline]Ax = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]. We will either have exactly one solution (and that solution will be [mathjaxinline]\mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline]), or we will have infinitely many solutions.
After row reduction, we obtain [mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{ccc} 1 & 0 & -1 \\ 0 & 1 & 2 \\ 0 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & 0 \end{array} \right].[/mathjaxinline]
We have only two pivots. The third column represents a free variable, so the equation has infinitely many solutions. Note that, in the product [mathjaxinline]A x[/mathjaxinline], the columns of the matrix [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] are vectors that are combined with the entries of [mathjaxinline]x[/mathjaxinline] as the weights: [mathjaxinline]Ax = a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + a_3v_3[/mathjaxinline]. We have therefore shown that there are infinitely many sets of coefficients [mathjaxinline]\{ a_1; a_2; a_3\}[/mathjaxinline] that satisfy [mathjaxinline]a_1v_1 + a_2v_2 + a_3v_3 = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline], therefore the list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; v_3\}[/mathjaxinline] is linearly dependent.

What goes in the blank to make a correct statement?

If the matrix [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] yields [mathjaxinline]\underline{\; \; \; \; \; \; \; \; \; \; }[/mathjaxinline] when row-reduced, then the columns of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] cannot be linearly independent.

a row of all zeros a free variable either of the above

Answer:

A free variable implies [mathjaxinline]Ax = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline] has infinitely many solutions, and thus that the columns of [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] are not linearly independent.

The matrix [mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{cc} 1 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \\ 0 & 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline] has a row of all zeros, but its columns are still linearly independent.

What goes in the blank to make a correct statement?

If the matrix [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] has [mathjaxinline]\underline{\; \; \; \; \; \; \; \; \; \; }[/mathjaxinline], then there must be a free variable.

more rows than columns more columns than rows

Answer:

If there are more columns than rows, there can't be a pivot in every column, so there must be a free variable.

Putting the previous two statements together, what goes in the blank to make a correct statement?

A list of [mathjaxinline]\underline{\; \; \; \; \; \; \; \; \; \; }[/mathjaxinline] cannot be linearly independent.

3 vectors in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^5[/mathjaxinline] 5 vectors in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^3[/mathjaxinline]

Answer: A list of 5 vectors in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^3[/mathjaxinline] can be written as the columns of a [mathjaxinline]3\times 5[/mathjaxinline] matrix, which has more columns than rows. Therefore it will have a free variable, which means that the columns of the matrix are linearly dependent.

We have just shown the following results:

Proposition. If [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] is an [mathjaxinline]m\times n[/mathjaxinline] matrix with columns [mathjaxinline]v_1, v_2, \ldots v_ n \in \mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline], then the list [mathjaxinline]\{ v_1; v_2; \ldots v_ n\}[/mathjaxinline] is linearly independent if and only if [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] does not have a free variable.

If [mathjaxinline]n>m[/mathjaxinline], then any list of [mathjaxinline]n[/mathjaxinline] vectors in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^ m[/mathjaxinline] must be linearly dependent.

Without doing any row-reduction, you should be able to pick out three of the following lists of vectors that are linearly dependent. Which three?

  • List A: [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{array} \right]; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{array} \right]; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 0 \\ 1 \end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline]

  • List B: [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 0 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 5 \\ 7 \end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline]

  • List C: [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 1 \\ 4\end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 13 \\ 3 \\ 7\end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 1 \\ 5 \\ -3\end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 15 \\ -2 \\ 6 \\ 2\end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 5 \\ 3 \\ 8 \\ 11\end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline]

  • List D: [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 3 \\ 5 \\ 7 \\ 11 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \\ 4 \\ 5 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 2 \\ 4 \\ 6 \\ 8 \\ 10 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \\ 5 \end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline]

  • List E: [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 1 \\ 0\\ 2 \\ 3 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 2 \\ 0\\ 7 \\ 1 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 0 \\ 12 \\ 0\\ 23 \\ 35 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline]

  • List F: [mathjaxinline]\left\{ \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 1 \\ 1 \\ 1 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 1 \\ 2 \\ 1 \end{array} \right] ; \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 1 \\ 1 \\ 2 \end{array} \right] \right\}[/mathjaxinline]

A B C D E F

Answer:

Choice B has the zero vector and is therefore linearly dependent. Choice C has 5 vectors in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^4[/mathjaxinline] and is therefore linearly dependent (expressed as a matrix, there would be more columns than rows). Choice E has a pair of collinear vectors: the third vector is twice the second vector. The list in choice E is therefore linearly dependent.

  • Recognize basic properties of linear transformations.

For time budgeting purposes, this sequence has 4 videos totaling 19 minutes, plus some questions.

Define the function [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] by [mathjaxinline]T(x) = Ax[/mathjaxinline], where [mathjaxinline]A = \left[ \begin{array}{ccc} 6 & 5 & 3 \\ 2 & 4 & 23 \end{array} \right].[/mathjaxinline]

What is the domain of [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline]?

[mathjaxinline]\mathbb R[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^3[/mathjaxinline]

What is the codomain of [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline]?

[mathjaxinline]\mathbb R[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline] [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^3[/mathjaxinline]

Answer:

[mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] has 3 columns. Therefore, for the product [mathjaxinline]Ax[/mathjaxinline] to be defined, [mathjaxinline]x[/mathjaxinline] must have 3 rows, and will be a vector in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^3[/mathjaxinline].
Since [mathjaxinline]A[/mathjaxinline] has two rows, the product [mathjaxinline]Ax[/mathjaxinline] will be a vector in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline].

Definition. Let [mathjaxinline]V,W[/mathjaxinline] be vector spaces over a field [mathjaxinline]F[/mathjaxinline]. A function [mathjaxinline]T: V\rightarrow W[/mathjaxinline] is a linear transformation if both of the following properties hold:

  • For any vectors [mathjaxinline]v, w[/mathjaxinline] in the domain, [mathjaxinline]T(v + w) = T(v) + T(w).[/mathjaxinline]

  • For any vector [mathjaxinline]v[/mathjaxinline] in the domain, and any scalar [mathjaxinline]a\in F[/mathjaxinline], [mathjaxinline]T(av) = aT(v)[/mathjaxinline].

Informally, if [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] satisfies the first condition we say that ‚Äú[mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respects vector addition", and if [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] satisfies the second condition we say that ‚Äú[mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respects scalar multiplication."

Define [mathjaxinline]T: \mathbb R^3 \rightarrow \mathbb R^3[/mathjaxinline] by [mathjaxinline]T(v) = 2v[/mathjaxinline].

Does [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respect vector addition? That is, is [mathjaxinline]T(v + w) = T(v) + T(w)[/mathjaxinline] for all vectors [mathjaxinline]v,w[/mathjaxinline]? Try a couple of specific numerical examples if you're not sure.

Does [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respect scalar multiplication? That is, is [mathjaxinline]T(av) = aT(v)[/mathjaxinline] for all vectors [mathjaxinline]v[/mathjaxinline] and scalars [mathjaxinline]a[/mathjaxinline]? Try a couple of specific numerical examples if you're not sure.

Is [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] a linear transformation?

Answer:

It appears that the transformation is linear. We can prove it with algebra:

We have [mathjaxinline]T(v + w) = 2(v+w) = 2v + 2w = T(v) + T(w)[/mathjaxinline]. Therefore [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respects vector addition.

Similarly [mathjaxinline]T(av) = 2(av) = a(2v) = aT(v),[/mathjaxinline] so [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respects scalar multiplication. Thus, [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] is a linear transformation.

Define [mathjaxinline]T: \mathbb R^2 \rightarrow \mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline] by [mathjaxinline]T(v) = \mathbf{0}[/mathjaxinline].

Does [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respect vector addition? That is, is [mathjaxinline]T(v + w) = T(v) + T(w)[/mathjaxinline] for all vectors [mathjaxinline]v, w[/mathjaxinline]? Try a couple of specific numerical examples if you're not sure.

Does [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respect scalar multiplication? That is, is [mathjaxinline]T(av) = aT(v)[/mathjaxinline] for all vectors [mathjaxinline]v[/mathjaxinline] and scalars [mathjaxinline]a[/mathjaxinline]? Try a couple of specific numerical examples if you're not sure.

Is [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] a linear transformation?

Answer:

[mathjaxinline]T(v+w) = \mathbf{0}= \mathbf{0}+ \mathbf{0}= T(v) + T(w)[/mathjaxinline], so [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respects addition. Also, [mathjaxinline]T(av) = \mathbf{0}= a\mathbf{0}= aT(v)[/mathjaxinline], so [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respects scalar multiplication.

Hence [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] is a linear transformation.

Define [mathjaxinline]T: \mathbb R^2 \rightarrow \mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline] by [mathjaxinline]T(v) = \left[\begin{array}{c} 4 \\ 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline].

Does [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respect vector addition? That is, is [mathjaxinline]T(v + w) = T(v) + T(w)[/mathjaxinline] for all vectors [mathjaxinline]v,w[/mathjaxinline]? Try a couple of specific numerical examples if you're not sure.

Does [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respect scalar multiplication? That is, is [mathjaxinline]T(av) = aT(v)[/mathjaxinline] for all vectors [mathjaxinline]v[/mathjaxinline] and scalars [mathjaxinline]a[/mathjaxinline]? Try a couple of specific numerical examples if you're not sure.

Is [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] a linear transformation?

Answer:

Here, trying specific vectors provides a counterexample, which is sufficient to show that [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] is not a linear transformation.
Let [mathjaxinline]v_1 = \left[ \begin{array}{c} 1\\ 2\\ 5 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline], [mathjaxinline]v_2 = \left[ \begin{array}{c} -4\\ 0\\ 9 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]
[mathjaxinline]T(v_1) + T(v_2) = \left[\begin{array}{c} 4 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] + \left[\begin{array}{c} 4 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] = \left[\begin{array}{c} 8 \\ 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]
Meanwhile, [mathjaxinline]T(v_1 + v_2) = \left[\begin{array}{c} 4 \\ 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]. [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] does not respect vector addition and is not linear.

We also see that [mathjaxinline]8T(v_1) = 8 \left[\begin{array}{c} 4 \\ 0 \end{array} \right] = \left[\begin{array}{c} 32 \\ 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]
Meanwhile, [mathjaxinline]T(8v_1) = \left[\begin{array}{c} 4 \\ 0 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]. [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] does not respect scalar multiplication; this on its own would also be enough to know that [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] is not linear.

Define [mathjaxinline]T: \mathbb {P} \rightarrow \mathbb R[/mathjaxinline] by [mathjaxinline]T(f) = f(1)[/mathjaxinline].

To get a sense of how [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] works, let's do a quick example. Suppose [mathjaxinline]f[/mathjaxinline] is the element of [mathjaxinline]\mathbb {P}[/mathjaxinline] given by [mathjaxinline]f(t) = 2t^2 -5[/mathjaxinline]. What is [mathjaxinline]T(f)[/mathjaxinline]?

Answer:

[mathjaxinline]T(f) = f(1) = 2\cdot 1^2 - 5 = -3.[/mathjaxinline]

Does [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respect vector addition? That is, is [mathjaxinline]T(f+g) = T(f) + T(g)[/mathjaxinline] for all vectors [mathjaxinline]f,g \in \mathbb {P}[/mathjaxinline]? Try a couple of specific numerical examples if you're not sure.

Does [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respect scalar multiplication? That is, is [mathjaxinline]T(af) = aT(f)[/mathjaxinline] for all vectors [mathjaxinline]f \in \mathbb {P}[/mathjaxinline] and scalars [mathjaxinline]a[/mathjaxinline]? Try a couple of specific numerical examples if you're not sure.

Is [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] a linear transformation?

Answer:

[mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respects vector addition because:
[mathjaxinline]f(1) + g(1) = (f+g)(1)[/mathjaxinline]
i.e. we can evaluate polynomials at 1 separately and add the results, or we can add the polynomials and evaluate the result at 1, and we'll get the same result.

[mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] respects scalar multiplication because:
[mathjaxinline]cT(f) = cf(1) = (cf)(1) = T(cf)[/mathjaxinline]
i.e. we can evaluate a polynomial at 1 and multiply the result by a scalar, or multiply the polynomial by the scalar first and then evaluate the result at 1, and we'll get the same result.

Suppose [mathjaxinline]T: \mathbb {P} \rightarrow \mathbb R^2[/mathjaxinline] is a linear transformation. Let [mathjaxinline]f,g\in \mathbb {P}[/mathjaxinline] be the polynomials given by [mathjaxinline]f(t) = t^2[/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]g(t) = t[/mathjaxinline].

Suppose that [mathjaxinline]T(f) = \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline] and [mathjaxinline]T(g) = \left[\begin{array}{c} 2 \\ -1 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline]

If [mathjaxinline]h \in \mathbb {P}[/mathjaxinline] is the polynomial given by [mathjaxinline]h(t) = -2t^2[/mathjaxinline], what must [mathjaxinline]T(h)[/mathjaxinline] be?

To enter a vector in [mathjaxinline]\mathbb R^ n[/mathjaxinline] such as [mathjaxinline]\left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \\ 3 \end{array} \right][/mathjaxinline], you can either:

  • enter it as you would a [mathjaxinline]3\times 1[/mathjaxinline] matrix: [[1],[2],[3]]

  • or, you can enter it as <1,2,3>

Use decimals only.

If [mathjaxinline]j \in \mathbb {P}[/mathjaxinline] is the polynomial given by [mathjaxinline]h(t) = 3t^2-t[/mathjaxinline], what must [mathjaxinline]T(j)[/mathjaxinline] be?

Answer:

We are assured that [mathjaxinline]T[/mathjaxinline] is a linear transformation, so we have:

[mathjax]T(h) = T(-2t^2) = -2T(t^2) = -2T(f) = -2\left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \end{array} \right] = \left[\begin{array}{c} -2 \\ -4 \end{array} \right].[/mathjax]

Also,

[mathjax]T(j) = T(3t^2-t) = T(3t^2) + T(-t) = 3T(t^2) + -T(t) = 3T(f) - T(g) = 3\left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 2 \end{array} \right] - \left[\begin{array}{c} 2 \\ -1 \end{array} \right] = \left[\begin{array}{c} 1 \\ 7 \end{array} \right].[/mathjax]
